1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Lebanon_as_envisaged_by_French_General_Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_in_1862.jpg/340px-Lebanon_as_envisaged_by_French_General_Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_in_1862.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1820" data-file-height="2400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lebanon_as_envisaged_by_French_General_Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul_in_1862.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1862 map drawn by the French expedition of <a href="/wiki/Charles-Marie-Napol%C3%A9on_de_Beaufort_d%27Hautpoul" title="Charles-Marie-Napoléon de Beaufort d&#39;Hautpoul">Beaufort d'Hautpoul</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Hakim2013_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hakim2013-36">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> later used as a template for the 1920 borders of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Lebanon" title="Greater Lebanon">Greater Lebanon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Firro2003_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firro2003-37">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rooke_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rooke-38">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>During this period Lebanon was divided into several provinces: Northern and Southern Mount Lebanon, Tripoli, Baalbek and Beqaa Valley and <a href="/wiki/Jabal_Amel" title="Jabal Amel">Jabal Amel</a>.
In southern Mount Lebanon in 1590, <a href="/wiki/Fakhr-al-Din_II" title="Fakhr-al-Din II">Fakhr-al-Din II</a> became the successor to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon_Emirate#Qurqumaz_(1544–1585)" title="Mount Lebanon Emirate">Korkmaz</a>. He soon established his authority as paramount prince of the Druze in the Shouf area of Mount Lebanon. Eventually, Fakhr-al-Din II was appointed <a href="/wiki/Sanjak-bey" title="Sanjak-bey">Sanjakbey</a> (Governor) of several Ottoman sub-provinces, with responsibility for tax-gathering. He extended his control over a substantial part of Mount Lebanon and its coastal area, even building a fort as far inland as <a href="/wiki/Palmyra_Castle" title="Palmyra Castle">Palmyra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> This over-reaching eventually became too much for Ottoman Sultan <a href="/wiki/Murad_IV" title="Murad IV">Murad IV</a>, who sent a punitive expedition to capture him in 1633. He was taken to <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, kept in prison for two years and then executed along with one of his sons in April 1635.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Surviving members of Fakhr al-Din's family ruled a reduced area under closer Ottoman control until the end of the 17th century.
</p><p>On the death of the last Maan emir, various members of the Shihab clan ruled Mount Lebanon until 1830. Approximately 10,000 Christians <a href="/wiki/1860_Lebanon_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="1860 Lebanon conflict">were killed</a> by the Druzes during inter-communal violence in 1860.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Shortly afterwards, the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Mount_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Emirate of Mount Lebanon">Emirate of Mount Lebanon</a>, which lasted about 400 years, was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon_Mutasarrifate" title="Mount Lebanon Mutasarrifate">Mount Lebanon Mutasarrifate</a>, as a result of a European-Ottoman treaty called the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A8glement_Organique_(Lebanon)" class="mw-redirect" title="Règlement Organique (Lebanon)">Règlement Organique</a>.
The Baalbek and Beqaa Valley and Jabal Amel was ruled intermittently by various Shia feudal families, especially the Al Ali Alsagheer in Jabal Amel that remained in power until 1865  when Ottomans took direct ruling of the region. <a href="/wiki/Youssef_Bey_Karam" title="Youssef Bey Karam">Youssef Bey Karam</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> a Lebanese nationalist played an influential role in Lebanon's independence during this era.
</p><p>In 1920, following <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the area of the Mutasarrifate, plus some surrounding areas which were predominantly <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia Islam in Lebanon">Shia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunni</a>, became a part of the state of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Lebanon" title="Greater Lebanon">Greater Lebanon</a> under the <a href="/wiki/French_Mandate_of_Syria_and_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="French Mandate of Syria and Lebanon">French Mandate of Syria and Lebanon</a>. Around 100,000 people in Beirut and Mount Lebanon died of starvation during World War I.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In the first half of 1920, Lebanese territory was claimed as part of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Kingdom_of_Syria" title="Arab Kingdom of Syria">Arab Kingdom of Syria</a>, but shortly the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Syrian_War" title="Franco-Syrian War">Franco-Syrian War</a> resulted in Arab defeat and capitulation of the Hashemites.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg/220px-French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg/330px-French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg/440px-French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="922" data-file-height="762" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon_map_en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the <a href="/wiki/Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon" title="Mandate for Syria and the Lebanon">French Mandate</a> and the states created in 1920</div></div></div>
<p>On 1 September 1920, France reestablished <a href="/wiki/Greater_Lebanon" title="Greater Lebanon">Greater Lebanon</a> after the Moutasarrifiya rule removed several regions belonging to the Principality of Lebanon and gave them to Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Lebanon was a largely Christian country (mainly <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Christianity in Lebanon">Maronite</a> territory with some <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Christianity in Lebanon">Greek Orthodox</a> enclaves) but it also included areas containing many Muslims and <a href="/wiki/Druze_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Druze in Lebanon">Druze</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> On 1 September 1926, France formed the Lebanese Republic. A constitution was adopted on 25 May 1926 establishing a democratic republic with a parliamentary system of government.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_from_France">Independence from France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Independence from France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beirut%27s_Martyrs%27_Square_during_celebrations_marking_the_release_by_the_French_of_Lebanon%27s_government_from_Rashayya_prison_on_November_22,_1943,_the_day_of_Lebanon%27s_independence._Adib_Ibrahim.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Beirut%27s_Martyrs%27_Square_during_celebrations_marking_the_release_by_the_French_of_Lebanon%27s_government_from_Rashayya_prison_on_November_22%2C_1943%2C_the_day_of_Lebanon%27s_independence._Adib_Ibrahim.jpg/170px-thumbnail.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Beirut%27s_Martyrs%27_Square_during_celebrations_marking_the_release_by_the_French_of_Lebanon%27s_government_from_Rashayya_prison_on_November_22%2C_1943%2C_the_day_of_Lebanon%27s_independence._Adib_Ibrahim.jpg/255px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Beirut%27s_Martyrs%27_Square_during_celebrations_marking_the_release_by_the_French_of_Lebanon%27s_government_from_Rashayya_prison_on_November_22%2C_1943%2C_the_day_of_Lebanon%27s_independence._Adib_Ibrahim.jpg/340px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1041" data-file-height="1836" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beirut%27s_Martyrs%27_Square_during_celebrations_marking_the_release_by_the_French_of_Lebanon%27s_government_from_Rashayya_prison_on_November_22,_1943,_the_day_of_Lebanon%27s_independence._Adib_Ibrahim.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Martyrs%27_Square,_Beirut" title="Martyrs&#39; Square, Beirut">Martyrs' Square in Beirut</a> during celebrations marking the release by the French of Lebanon's government from <a href="/wiki/Rashayya_prison" class="mw-redirect" title="Rashayya prison">Rashayya prison</a> on 22 November 1943</div></div></div>
<p>Lebanon gained a measure of independence while France was occupied by Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> General <a href="/wiki/Henri_Dentz" title="Henri Dentz">Henri Dentz</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy</a> <a href="/wiki/High_Commissioner" class="mw-redirect" title="High Commissioner">High Commissioner</a> for Syria and Lebanon, played a major role in the independence of the nation. The Vichy authorities in 1941 allowed Germany to move aircraft and supplies through <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> where they were used against British forces. The United Kingdom, fearing that <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> would gain full control of Lebanon and <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> by pressure on the weak Vichy government, sent its army into Syria and Lebanon.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>After the fighting ended in Lebanon, General <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> visited the area. Under political pressure from both inside and outside Lebanon, de Gaulle recognized the independence of Lebanon. On 26 November 1941 General <a href="/wiki/Georges_Catroux" title="Georges Catroux">Georges Catroux</a> announced that Lebanon would become independent under the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Free_French" class="mw-redirect" title="Free French">Free French</a> government. Elections were held in 1943 and on 8 November 1943 the new Lebanese government unilaterally abolished the mandate. The French reacted by imprisoning the new government. In the face of international pressure, the French released the government officials on 22 November 1943. The <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">allies</a> occupied the region until the end of World War II.
</p><p>Following the end of World War II in Europe the French mandate may be said to have been terminated without any formal action on the part of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> or its successor the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>. The mandate was ended by the declaration of the mandatory power, and of the new states themselves, of their independence, followed by a process of piecemeal unconditional recognition by other powers, culminating in formal admission to the United Nations. Article 78 of the UN Charter ended the status of tutelage for any member state: "The trusteeship system shall not apply to territories which have become Members of the United Nations, relationship among which shall be based on respect for the principle of sovereign equality."<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> So when the UN officially came into existence on 24 October 1945, after ratification of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Charter" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Charter">United Nations Charter</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_members_of_the_United_Nations_Security_Council" title="Permanent members of the United Nations Security Council">five permanent members</a>, as both Syria and Lebanon were founding member states, the French mandate for both was legally terminated on that date and full independence attained.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The last French troops withdrew in December 1946.
</p><p>Lebanon's unwritten <a href="/wiki/National_Pact" title="National Pact">National Pact</a> of 1943 required that its president be Maronite Christian, its <a href="/wiki/Speaker_(politics)" title="Speaker (politics)">speaker of the parliament</a> to be a <a href="/wiki/Shi%27a_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shi&#39;a Islam in Lebanon">Shiite Muslim</a>, its prime minister be <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunni Muslim</a>, and the Deputy Speaker of Parliament and the Deputy Prime Minister be <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Christianity in Lebanon">Greek Orthodox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet,_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg/220px-Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg/330px-Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg/440px-Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet%2C_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2593" data-file-height="2577" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Straatbeeld_in_Beiroet,_Bestanddeelnr_255-6182.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beirut in 1950</div></div></div>
<p>Lebanon's history since independence has been marked by alternating periods of political stability and turmoil interspersed with prosperity built on <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>'s position as a regional center for finance and trade.<sup id="cite_ref-dos-2009-01_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dos-2009-01-49">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1948, Lebanon supported neighbouring Arab countries in a <a href="/wiki/1948_Arab%E2%80%93Israeli_War" title="1948 Arab–Israeli War">war against Israel</a>. While some irregular forces crossed the border and carried out minor skirmishes against Israel, it was without the support of the Lebanese government, and Lebanese troops did not officially invade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris2008524_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris2008524-50">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Lebanon agreed to support the forces with covering artillery fire, armored cars, volunteers and logistical support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris2008259_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris2008259-51">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> On 5–6 June 1948, the Lebanese army – led by the then <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Defense" class="mw-redirect" title="Minister of Defense">Minister of National Defence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emir_Majid_Arslan_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Emir Majid Arslan II">Emir Majid Arslan</a> – captured <a href="/wiki/Al-Malkiyya" title="Al-Malkiyya">Al-Malkiyya</a>. This was Lebanon's only success in the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorris2008260_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorris2008260-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>100,000 <a href="/wiki/Palestinians_in_Lebanon" title="Palestinians in Lebanon">Palestinians</a> fled to Lebanon because of the war. Israel <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_right_of_return" title="Palestinian right of return">did not permit their return</a> after the cease-fire.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-53">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> As of 2017 between 174,000 and 450,000 Palestinian refugees live in Lebanon with about half in refugee camps (although these are often decades old and resemble neighborhoods).<sup id="cite_ref-aljazeera_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeera-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Palestinians  often cannot obtain <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_nationality_law" title="Lebanese nationality law">Lebanese citizenship</a> or even <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_identity_card" title="Lebanese identity card">Lebanese identity cards</a> so many are legally barred from owning property or performing certain occupations (including law, medicine, and engineering).<sup id="cite_ref-Butters_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Butters-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a>, Palestinian refugees in Lebanon live in "appalling social and economic conditions."
</p><p>In 1958, during the last months of President <a href="/wiki/Camille_Chamoun" title="Camille Chamoun">Camille Chamoun</a>'s term, <a href="/wiki/1958_Lebanon_crisis" title="1958 Lebanon crisis">an insurrection</a> broke out, instigated by Lebanese Muslims who wanted to make Lebanon a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Republic" title="United Arab Republic">United Arab Republic</a>. Chamoun requested assistance, and 5,000 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marines" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Marines">United States Marines</a> were briefly dispatched to Beirut on 15 July. After the crisis, a new government was formed, led by the popular former general <a href="/wiki/Fuad_Chehab" title="Fuad Chehab">Fuad Chehab</a>.
</p><p>With the <a href="/wiki/Black_September_in_Jordan" class="mw-redirect" title="Black September in Jordan">defeat</a> of the <a href="/wiki/PLO" class="mw-redirect" title="PLO">PLO</a> in Jordan, many Palestinian militants relocated to Lebanon, increasing their <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_insurgency_in_South_Lebanon" title="Palestinian insurgency in South Lebanon">armed campaign</a> against Israel. The relocation of Palestinian bases also led to increasing sectarian tensions between Palestinians versus the Maronites and other Lebanese factions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war_and_occupation">Civil war and occupation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Civil war and occupation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Civil_War" title="Lebanese Civil War">Lebanese Civil War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Green_Line,_Beirut_1982.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg/220px-Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg/330px-Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg/440px-Green_Line%2C_Beirut_1982.jpg 2x" data-file-width="752" data-file-height="544" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Green_Line,_Beirut_1982.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Green_Line_(Lebanon)" title="Green Line (Lebanon)">Green Line</a> that separated west and east Beirut, 1982</div></div></div>
<p>In 1975, following increasing sectarian tensions, a full-scale civil war broke out in Lebanon. The <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Civil_War" title="Lebanese Civil War">Lebanese Civil War</a> pitted a coalition of Christian groups against the joint forces of the PLO, left-wing Druze and Muslim militias. In June 1976 Lebanese President <a href="/wiki/Elias_Sarkis" class="mw-redirect" title="Elias Sarkis">Elias Sarkis</a> asked for the Syrian Army to intervene on the side of the Christians and help restore peace.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> In October 1976 the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> agreed to establish a predominantly Syrian <a href="/wiki/Arab_Deterrent_Force" title="Arab Deterrent Force">Arab Deterrent Force</a>, which was charged with restoring calm.<sup id="cite_ref-fco-cpl_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fco-cpl-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1982, the <a href="/wiki/PLO_in_Lebanon" title="PLO in Lebanon">PLO</a> attacks from Lebanon on Israel led to <a href="/wiki/1982_Lebanon_War" title="1982 Lebanon War">an Israeli invasion</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Multinational_Force_in_Lebanon" title="Multinational Force in Lebanon">multinational force</a> of American, French and Italian contingents (joined in 1983 by a British contingent) were deployed in Beirut after <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Beirut" title="Siege of Beirut">the Israeli siege of the city</a>, to supervise the evacuation of the PLO. It returned in September 1982 after the assassination of <a href="/wiki/Bashir_Gemayel" class="mw-redirect" title="Bashir Gemayel">Bashir Gemayel</a> and subsequent fighting. During this time a number of massacres occurred, such as in  <a href="/wiki/Sabra_and_Shatila_massacre" title="Sabra and Shatila massacre">Sabra and Shatila</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_camps" class="mw-redirect" title="War of the camps">several refugee camps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The multinational force was withdrawn in the spring of 1984, following <a href="/wiki/1983_Beirut_barracks_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="1983 Beirut barracks bombing">a devastating bombing attack</a> during the previous year.
</p><p>In September 1988, the Parliament failed to elect a successor to President Gemayel as a result of differences between the Christians, Muslims, and Syrians. The Arab League Summit of May 1989 led to the formation of a Saudi–Moroccan–Algerian committee to solve the crisis. On 16 September 1989 the committee issued a peace plan which was accepted by all. A ceasefire was established, the ports and airports were re-opened and refugees began to return.<sup id="cite_ref-fco-cpl_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fco-cpl-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the same month, the Lebanese Parliament agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Taif_Agreement" title="Taif Agreement">Taif Agreement</a>, which included an outline timetable for Syrian withdrawal from Lebanon and a formula for the de-confessionalisation of the Lebanese political system.<sup id="cite_ref-fco-cpl_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fco-cpl-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The war ended at the end of 1990 after sixteen years; it had caused massive loss of human life and property, and devastated the country's economy. It is estimated that 150,000 people were killed and another 200,000 wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Nearly a million civilians were displaced by the war, and some never returned.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Parts of Lebanon were left in ruins.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The Taif Agreement has still not been implemented in full and Lebanon's political system continues to be divided along sectarian lines.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Withdrawal_and_aftermath">Withdrawal and aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Withdrawal and aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Syrian_occupation_of_Lebanon" title="Syrian occupation of Lebanon">Syrian occupation of Lebanon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Israeli_occupation_of_Southern_Lebanon" title="Israeli occupation of Southern Lebanon">Israeli occupation of Southern Lebanon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg/170px-Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg/255px-Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8c/Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg 2x" data-file-width="305" data-file-height="410" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Demonstration_14_March_2005_31.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Demonstrators calling for the withdrawal of Syrian forces.</div></div></div>
<p>The internal political situation in Lebanon significantly changed in the early <a href="/wiki/2000s_(decade)" title="2000s (decade)">2000s</a>. After the Israeli withdrawal from southern Lebanon and the death of <a href="/wiki/Hafez_Al-Assad" class="mw-redirect" title="Hafez Al-Assad">Hafez Al-Assad</a> in 2000, the Syrian military presence faced criticism and resistance from the Lebanese population.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 14 February 2005, former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Rafik_Hariri" class="mw-redirect" title="Rafik Hariri">Rafik Hariri</a> was assassinated in a <a href="/wiki/Car_bomb" title="Car bomb">car bomb</a> explosion.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Leaders of the <a href="/wiki/March_14_Alliance" title="March 14 Alliance">March 14 Alliance</a> accused <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> of the attack,<sup id="cite_ref-Syria-assassin_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Syria-assassin-65">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> while Syria and the <a href="/wiki/March_8_Alliance" title="March 8 Alliance">March 8 Alliance</a> claimed that <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> was behind the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The Hariri assassination marked the beginning of a series of assassinations that resulted in the death of many prominent Lebanese figures.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;nb 5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The assassination triggered the <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Revolution" title="Cedar Revolution">Cedar Revolution</a>, a series of demonstrations which demanded the withdrawal of Syrian troops from Lebanon and the establishment of an international commission to investigate the assassination. Under pressure from the West, Syria began withdrawing,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and by 26 April 2005 all Syrian soldiers had returned to Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1595" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1595">UNSC Resolution 1595</a> called for an investigation into the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/UN_International_Independent_Investigation_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="UN International Independent Investigation Commission">UN International Independent Investigation Commission</a> published preliminary findings on 20 October 2005 in the <a href="/wiki/Mehlis_report" title="Mehlis report">Mehlis report</a>, which cited indications that the assassination was organized by Syrian and Lebanese intelligence services.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_S2005662_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_S2005662-73">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 12 July 2006, <a href="/wiki/Hezbollah" title="Hezbollah">Hezbollah</a> launched a series of rocket attacks and <a href="/wiki/2006_Hezbollah_cross-border_raid" title="2006 Hezbollah cross-border raid">raids</a> into <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israeli</a> territory, where they killed three Israeli soldiers and captured two others.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Israel responded with <a href="/wiki/Airstrike" title="Airstrike">airstrikes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> fire on targets in Lebanon, and a ground invasion of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Lebanon" title="Southern Lebanon">southern Lebanon</a>, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/2006_Lebanon_War" title="2006 Lebanon War">2006 Lebanon War</a>. The conflict was officially ended by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1701" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1701">UNSC Resolution 1701</a> on 14 August 2006, which ordered a ceasefire.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Some 1,191 Lebanese<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and 160 Israelis<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> were <a href="/wiki/Casualties_of_the_2006_Lebanon_War" title="Casualties of the 2006 Lebanon War">killed</a> in the conflict. Beirut's southern suburb was heavily damaged by Israeli airstrikes.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tyre_air_strike.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Tyre_air_strike.jpg/220px-Tyre_air_strike.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Tyre_air_strike.jpg/330px-Tyre_air_strike.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Tyre_air_strike.jpg/440px-Tyre_air_strike.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1504" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tyre_air_strike.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dust rises after the impact of two bombs dropped during an <a href="/wiki/Israeli_Air_Force" title="Israeli Air Force">IAF</a> airstrike on <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>, 2006</div></div></div>
<p>In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Nahr_al-Bared" title="Nahr al-Bared">Nahr al-Bared</a> refugee camp became the center of the <a href="/wiki/2007_Lebanon_conflict" title="2007 Lebanon conflict">2007 Lebanon conflict</a> between the Lebanese Army and <a href="/wiki/Fatah_al-Islam" title="Fatah al-Islam">Fatah al-Islam</a>. At least 169 soldiers, 287 insurgents and 47 civilians were killed in the battle. Funds for the reconstruction of the area have been slow to materialize.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 2006 and 2008, <a href="/wiki/2006%E2%80%932008_Lebanese_political_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2006–2008 Lebanese political protests">a series of protests</a> led by groups opposed to the pro-Western Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Fouad_Siniora" title="Fouad Siniora">Fouad Siniora</a> demanded the creation of a national unity government, over which the mostly Shia opposition groups would have veto power. When <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Lahoud" title="Émile Lahoud">Émile Lahoud</a>'s presidential term ended in October 2007, the opposition refused to vote for a successor unless a power-sharing deal was reached, leaving Lebanon without a president.
</p><p>On 9 May 2008, <a href="/wiki/Hezbollah" title="Hezbollah">Hezbollah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amal_Movement" title="Amal Movement">Amal</a> forces, sparked by a government declaration that <a href="/wiki/Hezbollah" title="Hezbollah">Hezbollah</a>'s communications network was illegal, seized western <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Global_Politician_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Politician-81">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> leading to the <a href="/wiki/2008_conflict_in_Lebanon" title="2008 conflict in Lebanon">2008 conflict in Lebanon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The Lebanese government denounced the violence as a coup attempt.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> At least 62 people died in the resulting clashes between pro-government and opposition militias.<sup id="cite_ref-Doha_NYT_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doha_NYT-84">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> On 21 May 2008, the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Doha_Agreement" title="Doha Agreement">Doha Agreement</a> ended the fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-Global_Politician_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Politician-81">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Doha_NYT_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doha_NYT-84">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> As part of the accord, which ended 18 months of political paralysis,<sup id="cite_ref-Doha_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doha-85">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michel_Suleiman" title="Michel Suleiman">Michel Suleiman</a> became president and a national unity government was established, granting a veto to the opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-Global_Politician_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Politician-81">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The agreement was a victory for opposition forces, as the government caved in to all their main demands.<sup id="cite_ref-Doha_NYT_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doha_NYT-84">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In early January 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_government_of_November_2009" title="Lebanese government of November 2009">national unity government</a> collapsed due to growing tensions stemming from the <a href="/wiki/Special_Tribunal_for_Lebanon" title="Special Tribunal for Lebanon">Special Tribunal for Lebanon</a>, which was expected to indict Hezbollah members for the Hariri assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_collapse_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_collapse-86">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The parliament elected <a href="/wiki/Najib_Mikati" title="Najib Mikati">Najib Mikati</a>, the candidate for the Hezbollah-led <a href="/wiki/March_8_Alliance" title="March 8 Alliance">March 8 Alliance</a>, Prime Minister of Lebanon, making him responsible for forming a new government.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_collapse_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_collapse-87">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Hezbollah leader <a href="/wiki/Hassan_Nasrallah" title="Hassan Nasrallah">Hassan Nasrallah</a> insists that Israel was responsible for the assassination of Hariri.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> A report leaked by the <a href="/wiki/Al_Akhbar_(Lebanon)" title="Al Akhbar (Lebanon)">Al-Akhbar</a> newspaper in November 2010 stated that Hezbollah has drafted plans for a takeover of the country in the event that the Special Tribunal for Lebanon issues an indictment against its members.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_civil_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian civil war">Syrian civil war</a> threatened to spill over in Lebanon, causing more <a href="/wiki/Syrian_civil_war_spillover_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian civil war spillover in Lebanon">incidents of sectarian violence</a> and armed clashes between <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunnis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alawites_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Alawites in Lebanon">Alawites</a> in Tripoli.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/UNHCR" class="mw-redirect" title="UNHCR">UNHCR</a>, the number of Syrian refugees in Lebanon increased from around 250,000 in early 2013 to 1,000,000 in late 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-refugees_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-refugees-92">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, The <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Forces_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese Forces Party">Lebanese Forces Party</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kataeb_Party" title="Kataeb Party">Kataeb Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Free_Patriotic_Movement" title="Free Patriotic Movement">Free Patriotic Movement</a> voiced concerns that the country's sectarian based political system is being undermined by the influx of Syrian refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> On 6 May 2015, <a href="/wiki/UNHCR" class="mw-redirect" title="UNHCR">UNHCR</a> suspended registration of Syrian refugees at the request of the Lebanese government.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> In February 2016, the Lebanese government signed the Lebanon Compact, granting a minimum of €400 million of support for refugees and vulnerable Lebanese citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> As of October 2016, the government estimates that the country hosts 1.5 million Syrians.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Lebanon" title="Geography of Lebanon">Geography of Lebanon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Qadisha.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Qadisha.jpg/220px-Qadisha.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Qadisha.jpg/330px-Qadisha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Qadisha.jpg/440px-Qadisha.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Qadisha.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kadisha_Valley" title="Kadisha Valley">Kadisha Valley</a>, a gorge in northern Lebanon</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg/220px-Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg/330px-Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg/440px-Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg 2x" data-file-width="584" data-file-height="744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Satellite_image_of_Lebanon_in_March_2002.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lebanon from space. Snow cover can be seen on the western <a href="/wiki/Lebanon_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanon Mountains">Mount Lebanon</a> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Anti-Lebanon_Mountains" title="Anti-Lebanon Mountains">Anti-Lebanon</a> mountain ranges</div></div></div>
<p>Lebanon is located in Western Asia between latitudes <a href="/wiki/33rd_parallel_north" title="33rd parallel north">33°</a> and <a href="/wiki/35th_parallel_north" title="35th parallel north">35° N</a> and longitudes <a href="/wiki/35th_meridian_east" title="35th meridian east">35°</a> and <a href="/wiki/37th_meridian_east" title="37th meridian east">37° E</a>. Its land straddles the "northwest of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_plate" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabian plate">Arabian plate</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The country's surface area is 10,452 square kilometres (4,036&#160;sq&#160;mi) of which 10,230 square kilometres (3,950&#160;sq&#160;mi) is <a href="/wiki/Land_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Land area">land</a>. Lebanon has a coastline and border of 225 kilometres (140&#160;mi) on the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Mediterranean sea">Mediterranean sea</a> to the west, a 375 kilometres (233&#160;mi) border shared with <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> to the north and east and a 79 kilometres (49&#160;mi) long border with <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> to the south.<sup id="cite_ref-enbr_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enbr-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Blue_Line_(Lebanon)" title="Blue Line (Lebanon)">border</a> with the Israeli-occupied <a href="/wiki/Golan_Heights" title="Golan Heights">Golan Heights</a> is disputed by Lebanon in a small area called <a href="/wiki/Shebaa_Farms" class="mw-redirect" title="Shebaa Farms">Shebaa Farms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lebanon is divided into four distinct <a href="/wiki/Physical_geography" title="Physical geography">physiographic</a> regions: the coastal plain, the <a href="/wiki/Lebanon_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanon Mountains">Lebanon mountain</a> range, the <a href="/wiki/Beqaa_valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Beqaa valley">Beqaa valley</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Lebanon_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Lebanon mountains">Anti-Lebanon mountains</a>.
</p><p>The narrow and discontinuous <a href="/wiki/Coastal_plain" title="Coastal plain">coastal plain</a> stretches from the Syrian border in the north where it widens to form the <a href="/wiki/Akkar" class="mw-redirect" title="Akkar">Akkar</a> plain to <a href="/wiki/Naqoura" title="Naqoura">Ras al-Naqoura</a> at the border with Israel in the south. The fertile coastal plain is formed of marine sediments and river deposited <a href="/wiki/Alluvium" title="Alluvium">alluvium</a> alternating with sandy bays and rocky beaches.
The Lebanon mountains rise steeply parallel to the Mediterranean coast and form a ridge of <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> that runs for most of the country's length. The mountain range varies in width between 10&#160;km (6&#160;mi) and 56&#160;km (35&#160;mi); it is carved by narrow and deep gorges. The Lebanon mountains peak at 3,088 metres (10,131&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above mean sea level">above sea level</a> in <a href="/wiki/Qurnat_as_Sawda%27" title="Qurnat as Sawda&#39;">Qurnat as Sawda'</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Governorate" title="North Governorate">North Lebanon</a> and gradually slope to the south before rising again to a height of 2,695 metres (8,842&#160;ft) in <a href="/wiki/Mount_Sannine" title="Mount Sannine">Mount Sannine</a>. The Beqaa valley sits between the Lebanon mountains in the west and the Anti-Lebanon range in the east; it's a part of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Rift_Valley" title="Great Rift Valley">Great Rift Valley</a> system. The valley is 180&#160;km (112&#160;mi) long and 10 to 26&#160;km (6 to 16&#160;mi) wide, its fertile soil is formed by alluvial deposits. The Anti-Lebanon range runs parallel to the Lebanon mountains, its highest peak is in <a href="/wiki/Mount_Hermon" title="Mount Hermon">Mount Hermon</a> at 2,814 metres (9,232&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-enbr_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enbr-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The mountains of Lebanon are drained by <a href="/wiki/Intermittent_stream" class="mw-redirect" title="Intermittent stream">seasonal torrents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perennial_stream" title="Perennial stream">rivers</a> foremost of which is the 145 kilometres (90&#160;mi) long <a href="/wiki/Litani_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Litani river">Leontes</a> that rises in the Beqaa Valley to the west of <a href="/wiki/Baalbek" title="Baalbek">Baalbek</a> and empties into the Mediterranean Sea north of Tyre.<sup id="cite_ref-enbr_98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enbr-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Lebanon has <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_of_Lebanon" title="List of rivers of Lebanon">16 rivers</a> all of which are <a href="/wiki/Navigability" title="Navigability">non navigable</a>; 13 rivers originate from <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon" title="Mount Lebanon">Mount Lebanon</a> and run through the steep gorges and into the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, the other three arise in the <a href="/wiki/Beqaa_Valley" title="Beqaa Valley">Beqaa Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MOTE_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MOTE-100">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate of Lebanon">Climate of Lebanon</a></div>
<p>Lebanon has a moderate <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climate</a>. In coastal areas, winters are generally cool and rainy whilst summers are hot and humid. In more elevated areas, temperatures usually drop below freezing during the winter with heavy snow cover that remains until early summer on the higher mountaintops.<sup id="cite_ref-enbr_98-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enbr-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Although most of Lebanon receives a relatively large amount of rainfall, when measured annually in comparison to its arid surroundings, certain areas in north-eastern Lebanon receive little because of <a href="/wiki/Rain_shadow" title="Rain shadow">rain shadow</a> created by the high peaks of the western mountain range.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Lebanon" title="Wildlife of Lebanon">Wildlife of Lebanon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cedars02(js).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Cedars02%28js%29.jpg/220px-Cedars02%28js%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="337" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Cedars02%28js%29.jpg/330px-Cedars02%28js%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Cedars02%28js%29.jpg/440px-Cedars02%28js%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="816" data-file-height="1250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cedars02(js).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Cedrus_libani" title="Cedrus libani">Lebanon cedar</a> is the national emblem of Lebanon.</div></div></div>
<p>In ancient times, Lebanon was covered by large forests of <a href="/wiki/Cedrus_libani" title="Cedrus libani">cedar trees</a>, the national emblem of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-cedar_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cedar-103">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>  Millennia of deforestation have altered the hydrology in Mount Lebanon and changed the regional climate adversely.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> As of 2012, forests covered 13.4% of the Lebanese land area;<sup id="cite_ref-WB_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WB-105">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> they are under constant threat from <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a> caused by the long dry summer season.<sup id="cite_ref-IPSs_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPSs-106">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of longstanding exploitation, few old cedar trees remain in pockets of forests in Lebanon, but there is an active program to conserve and regenerate the forests. The Lebanese approach has emphasized natural regeneration over planting by creating the right conditions for <a href="/wiki/Germination" title="Germination">germination</a> and growth. The Lebanese state has created several nature reserves that contain cedars, including the <a href="/wiki/Al_Shouf_Cedar_Nature_Reserve" title="Al Shouf Cedar Nature Reserve">Shouf Biosphere Reserve</a>, the Jaj Cedar Reserve, the <a href="/wiki/Tannourine" title="Tannourine">Tannourine</a> Reserve, the Ammouaa and Karm Shbat Reserves in the Akkar district, and the Forest of the <a href="/wiki/Cedars_of_God" title="Cedars of God">Cedars of God</a> near <a href="/wiki/Bsharri" title="Bsharri">Bsharri</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, the Environment Ministry set a 10-year plan to increase the national forest coverage by 20%, which is equivalent to the planting of two million new trees each year.<sup id="cite_ref-dailystar1_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dailystar1-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The plan, which was funded by the United States Agency for International Development (<a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">USAID</a>), and implemented by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forest_Service" title="United States Forest Service">U.S. Forest Service</a> (USFS), through the Lebanon Reforestation Initiative (LRI), was inaugurated in 2011 by planting cedar, pine, wild almond, juniper, fir, oak and other seedlings, in ten regions around Lebanon.<sup id="cite_ref-dailystar1_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dailystar1-110">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> As of 2016, forests covered 13.6% of Lebanon, and other wooded lands represented a further 11%.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>  Since 2011, more than 600,000 trees, including cedars and other native species, have been planted throughout Lebanon as part of the Lebanon Reforestation Initiative (LRI).<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_issues">Environmental issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Environmental issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marine_environmental_issues_in_Lebanon" title="Marine environmental issues in Lebanon">Marine environmental issues in Lebanon</a></div>
<p>Beirut and Mount Lebanon have been facing a severe garbage crisis. After the closure of the Bourj Hammoud dump in 1997, the al-Naameh dumpsite was opened by the government in 1998. The al-Naameh dumpsite was planned to contain 2 million tons of waste for a limited period of six years at the most. It was designed to be a temporary solution, while the government would have devised a long-term plan. Sixteen years later al-Naameh was still open and exceeded its capacity by 13 million tons. In July 2015 the residents of the area, already protesting in the recent years, forced the closure of the dumpsite.The inefficiency of the government, as well as the corruption inside of the waste management company Sukleen in charge of managing the garbage in Lebanon, have resulted in piles of garbage blocking streets in Mount Lebanon and Beirut.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2015, the Lebanese government signed an agreement with Chinook Industrial Mining, part owned by <a href="/wiki/Chinook_Sciences" title="Chinook Sciences">Chinook Sciences</a>, to export over 100,000 tons of untreated waste from  <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a> and the surrounding area. The waste had accumulated in temporary locations following the government closure of the county's largest land fill site five months earlier. The contract was jointly signed with Howa International which has offices in Holland and Germany. The contract is reported to cost $212 per ton. The waste, which  is compacted and infectious, would have to be sorted and was estimated to be enough to fill 2,000 containers.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Initial reports that the waste was to be exported to <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> have been denied by diplomats.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2016, the government withdrew from negotiations after it was revealed that documents relating to the export of the trash to Russia were forgeries.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> On 19 March 2016, the Cabinet reopened the Naameh landfill for 60 days in line with a plan it passed few days earlier to end the trash crisis. The plan also stipulates the establishment of landfills in <a href="/wiki/Bourj_Hammoud" title="Bourj Hammoud">Bourj Hammoud</a> and Costa Brava, east and south of Beirut respectively. Sukleen trucks began removing piled garbage from Karantina and heading to Naameh. Environment Minister Mohammad Machnouk announced during a chat with activists that more than 8,000 tons of garbage had been collected so far as part of the government's trash plan in only 24 hours. The plan's execution is still ongoing.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, Human Rights Watch found that Lebanon's garbage crisis, and open burning of waste in particular, was posing a health risk to residents and violating the state's obligations under international law.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2018, Lebanon's parliament passed a law that banned open dumping and burning of waste. Despite penalties set in case of violations, Lebanese municipalities have been openly burning the waste, putting the lives of people in danger. In October 2018, <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a> researchers witnessed the open burning of dumps in al-Qantara and <a href="/wiki/Qabrikha" title="Qabrikha">Qabrikha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Government and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Lebanon" title="Politics of Lebanon">Politics of Lebanon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Lebanon" title="Human rights in Lebanon">Human rights in Lebanon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BeirutParliament.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/BeirutParliament.jpg/220px-BeirutParliament.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/BeirutParliament.jpg/330px-BeirutParliament.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/BeirutParliament.jpg/440px-BeirutParliament.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BeirutParliament.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Lebanese parliament">Lebanese parliament</a> building at the Place de l'Étoile</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beirut_protest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Beirut_protest.jpg/220px-Beirut_protest.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Beirut_protest.jpg/330px-Beirut_protest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Beirut_protest.jpg/440px-Beirut_protest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beirut_protest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of many protests in Beirut</div></div></div>
<p>Lebanon is a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_democracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary democracy">parliamentary democracy</a> that includes <a href="/wiki/Confessionalism_(politics)" title="Confessionalism (politics)">confessionalism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> in which high-ranking offices are reserved for members of specific religious groups. The <a href="/wiki/President_of_Lebanon" title="President of Lebanon">President</a>, for example, has to be a <a href="/wiki/Maronite_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Maronite Christianity in Lebanon">Maronite</a> Christian, the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Lebanon" title="Prime Minister of Lebanon">Prime Minister</a> a <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni Islam in Lebanon">Sunni Muslim</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_Parliament_of_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Speaker of the Parliament of Lebanon">Speaker of the Parliament</a> a <a href="/wiki/Shi%27a_Islam_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Shi&#39;a Islam in Lebanon">Shi’a Muslim</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister" class="mw-redirect" title="Deputy Prime Minister">Deputy Prime Minister</a> and the Deputy Speaker of Parliament <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Christianity_in_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox Christianity in Lebanon">Eastern Orthodox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-confessional_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-confessional-125">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> This system is intended to deter sectarian conflict and to represent fairly the demographic distribution of the 18 recognized religious groups in government.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until 1975, <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a> considered Lebanon to be one of only two (together with Israel) <a href="/wiki/Political_freedom" title="Political freedom">politically free</a> countries in the Middle East and North Africa region.<sup id="cite_ref-fh-historic_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fh-historic-129">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The country lost this status with the outbreak of the Civil War, and has not regained it since. Lebanon was rated "Partly Free" in 2013. Even so, the United States still considers Lebanon to be one of the most democratic nations in the Arab world.<sup id="cite_ref-fh-historic_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fh-historic-129">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until 2005, <a href="/wiki/Palestinians_in_Lebanon" title="Palestinians in Lebanon">Palestinians</a> were forbidden to work in over 70 jobs because they did not have <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_nationality_law" title="Lebanese nationality law">Lebanese citizenship</a>. After liberalization laws were passed in 2007, the number of banned jobs dropped to around 20.<sup id="cite_ref-amn_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amn-53">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, Palestinians were granted the same rights to work as other foreigners in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lebanon's national legislature is the <a href="/wiki/Unicameral" class="mw-redirect" title="Unicameral">unicameral</a> <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Lebanon" title="Parliament of Lebanon">Parliament of Lebanon</a>. Its 128 seats are <a href="/wiki/Reserved_political_positions" title="Reserved political positions">divided</a> equally between Christians and Muslims, proportionately between the 18 different denominations and proportionately between its 26 regions.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Prior to 1990, the ratio stood at 6:5 in favor of Christians; however, the <a href="/wiki/Taif_Agreement" title="Taif Agreement">Taif Agreement</a>, which put an end to the 1975–1990 civil war, adjusted the ratio to grant equal representation to followers of the two religions.<sup id="cite_ref-confessional_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-confessional-125">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Parliament is elected for a four-year term by popular vote on the basis of sectarian proportional representation.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The executive branch consists of the President, the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a>, and the Prime Minister, the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>. The parliament elects the president for a non-renewable six-year term by a two-third majority. The president appoints the Prime Minister,<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> following consultations with the parliament. The president and the prime minister form a cabinet, which must also adhere to the sectarian distribution set out by confessionalism.
</p><p>In an unprecedented move, the Lebanese parliament has extended its own term twice amid protests, the last being on 5 November 2014,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> an act which comes in direct contradiction with democracy and article #42 of the Lebanese constitution as no elections have taken place.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lebanon was without a President between May 2014 and October 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-Aoun_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aoun-135">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nationwide elections were finally scheduled for <a href="/wiki/2018_Lebanese_general_election" title="2018 Lebanese general election">May 2018</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of August 2019, the Lebanese cabinet included two ministers directly affiliated with <a href="/wiki/Hezbollah" title="Hezbollah">Hezbollah</a>, in addition to a close but officially non-member minister.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are 18 officially recognized religious groups in Lebanon, each with its own family law legislation and set of religious courts.<sup id="cite_ref-unesco1_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unesco1-139">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Lebanese legal system is based on the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_France" title="Law of France">French system</a>, and is a <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> country, with the exception for matters related to personal status (succession, marriage, divorce, adoption, etc.), which are governed by a separate set of laws designed for each sectarian community. For instance, the Islamic personal status laws are inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> law.<sup id="cite_ref-globalex_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-globalex-140">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> For Muslims, these tribunals deal with questions of marriage, divorce, custody, and inheritance and wills. For non-Muslims, personal status jurisdiction is split: the law of inheritance and wills falls under national civil jurisdiction, while Christian and Jewish religious courts are competent for marriage, divorce, and custody. Catholics can additionally appeal before the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rota" title="Roman Rota">Vatican Rota court</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most notable set of codified laws is the Code des Obligations et des Contrats promulgated in 1932 and equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/French_Civil_Code" class="mw-redirect" title="French Civil Code">French Civil Code</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-globalex_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-globalex-140">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">Capital punishment</a> is still de facto used to sanction certain crimes, but no longer enforced.<sup id="cite_ref-globalex_140-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-globalex-140">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Lebanese court system consists of three levels: courts of first instance, courts of appeal, and the court of cassation. The Constitutional Council rules on constitutionality of laws and electoral frauds. There also is a system of religious courts having jurisdiction over personal status matters within their own communities, with rules on matters such as marriage and inheritance.<sup id="cite_ref-Carnegie_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carnegie-142">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Lebanon" title="Foreign relations of Lebanon">Foreign relations of Lebanon</a></div>
<p>Lebanon concluded negotiations on an association agreement with the European Union in late 2001, and both sides initialed the accord in January 2002. It is included in the European Union's <a href="/wiki/European_Neighbourhood_Policy" title="European Neighbourhood Policy">European Neighbourhood Policy</a> (ENP), which aims at bringing the EU and its neighbours closer. Lebanon also has bilateral trade agreements with several Arab states and is working toward accession to the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>.
</p><p>Lebanon enjoys good relations with virtually all of the other Arab countries (despite historic tensions with Libya and Syria), and hosted an <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> Summit in March 2002 for the first time in more than 35 years. Lebanon is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Francophone" class="mw-redirect" title="Francophone">Francophone</a> countries and hosted the Francophone Summit in October 2002 as well as the <a href="/wiki/Jeux_de_la_Francophonie" title="Jeux de la Francophonie">Jeux de la Francophonie</a> in <a href="/wiki/2009_Jeux_de_la_Francophonie" title="2009 Jeux de la Francophonie">2009</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Armed_Forces" title="Lebanese Armed Forces">Lebanese Armed Forces</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg/220px-Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg/330px-Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg/440px-Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="992" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lebanese_Armed_Forces.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soldiers of the Lebanese army, 2009</div></div></div>
<p>The Lebanese Armed Forces (LAF) has 72,000 active personnel,<sup id="cite_ref-Lebanon_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lebanon-143">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> including 1,100 in the air force, and 1,000 in the navy.<sup id="cite_ref-Stinson_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stinson-144">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Lebanese Armed Forces' primary missions include defending Lebanon and its citizens against external aggression, maintaining internal stability and security, confronting threats against the country's vital interests, engaging in social development activities, and undertaking relief operations in coordination with public and humanitarian institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-lafmission_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lafmission-145">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lebanon is a major recipient of foreign military aid.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> With more than $400&#160;million since 2005, it is the second largest per capita recipient of <a href="/wiki/United_States_military_aid" title="United States military aid">American military aid</a> behind Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Human rights groups have repeatedly accused the Lebanese army of torture.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Human rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Lebanon" title="Human rights in Lebanon">Human rights in Lebanon</a></div>
<p>Discrimination against <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Lebanon" title="LGBT rights in Lebanon">LGBT people</a> in Lebanon is widespread.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> According to 2013 survey by the Pew Research Center, 80% of Lebanese respondents believe that <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexuality</a> should not be accepted by society.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lebanon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Governorates_of_Lebanon" title="Governorates of Lebanon">Governorates of Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Lebanon" title="Districts of Lebanon">Districts of Lebanon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="Municipalities of Lebanon">Municipalities of Lebanon</a></div>
<p>Lebanon is divided into eight <a href="/wiki/Governorate" title="Governorate">governorates</a> (<i>muḥāfaẓāt</i>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">محافظات</span>&#8206;; singular <i><a href="/wiki/Muhafazah" title="Muhafazah">muḥāfaẓah</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">محافظة</span>&#8206;) which are further subdivided into twenty-six districts (<i>aqdyah</i> <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">أقضية</span>&#8206;; singular: <i><a href="/wiki/Qadaa" class="mw-redirect" title="Qadaa">qadāʾ</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">قضاء</span>&#8206;).<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The districts themselves are also divided into several municipalities, each enclosing a group of cities or villages. The governorates and their respective districts are listed below:
</p>
<div role="img" class="floatright noresize" style="width: 360px; line-height: 1; text-align: center; background-color: #ffffff; position: relative;"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Lebanon_districts.png/360px-Lebanon_districts.png" decoding="async" width="360" height="444" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Lebanon_districts.png/540px-Lebanon_districts.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Lebanon_districts.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="740" />
